<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Secondary">Secondary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Secondary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A <b>secondary explosive</b> is less sensitive than a primary explosive and requires substantially more energy to be initiated. Because they are less sensitive, they are usable in a wider variety of applications and are safer to handle and store. Secondary explosives are used in larger quantities in an explosive train and are usually initiated by a smaller quantity of a primary explosive.
</p><p>Examples of secondary explosives include <a href="/wiki/TNT" title="TNT">TNT</a> and <a href="/wiki/RDX" title="RDX">RDX</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tertiary">Tertiary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Tertiary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Tertiary explosives</b>, also called <b>blasting agents</b>, are so insensitive to shock that they cannot be reliably detonated by practical quantities of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_explosive" title="Primary explosive">primary explosive</a>, and instead require an intermediate <a href="/wiki/Explosive_booster" title="Explosive booster">explosive booster</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_explosive" title="Secondary explosive">secondary explosive</a>. These are often used for safety and the typically lower costs of material and handling. The largest consumers are large-scale <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a> and <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a> operations.
</p><p>Most tertiaries include a fuel and an oxidizer. <a href="/wiki/ANFO" title="ANFO">ANFO</a> can be a tertiary explosive if its reaction rate is slow.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_velocity">By velocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: By velocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Low">Low</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Low">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Low explosives are compounds wherein the rate of decomposition proceeds through the material at less than the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a>. The decomposition is propagated by a flame front (<a href="/wiki/Deflagration" title="Deflagration">deflagration</a>) which travels much more slowly through the explosive material than a <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> of a high explosive. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">Under normal conditions</a>, low explosives undergo deflagration at rates that vary from a few centimetres per second to approximately 0.4 kilometres per second (1,300 ft/s). It is possible for them to deflagrate very quickly, producing an effect similar to a <a href="/wiki/Detonation" title="Detonation">detonation</a>. This can happen under higher <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> (such as when <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> deflagrates inside the confined space of a bullet casing, accelerating the bullet to well beyond the speed of sound) or <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>.
</p><p>A low explosive is usually a mixture of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combustible" title="Combustible">combustible</a> substance and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidant" title="Oxidant">oxidant</a> that decomposes rapidly (deflagration); however, they burn more slowly than a high explosive, which has an extremely fast burn rate.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Low explosives are normally employed as <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellants</a>. Included in this group are petroleum products such as <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_powder" title="Smokeless powder">smokeless powder</a>), and light <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnics</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Flare" title="Flare">flares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fireworks" title="Fireworks">fireworks</a>, but can replace high explosives in certain applications, including in gas pressure blasting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High">High</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: High">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>High explosives (HE) are explosive materials that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detonate" title="Detonate">detonate</a>, meaning that the <a href="/wiki/Explosion" title="Explosion">explosive</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shock_front" title="Shock front">shock front</a> passes through the material at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersonic" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> speed. High explosives detonate with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Explosive_velocity" title="Explosive velocity">explosive velocity</a> of about 3–9 kilometres per second (9,800–29,500 ft/s). For instance, TNT has a detonation (burn) rate of approximately 6.9 km/s (22,600 feet per second), detonating cord of 6.7 km/s (22,000 feet per second), and C-4 about 8.0 km/s (26,000 feet per second). They are normally employed in mining, demolition, and military applications. The term <i>high explosive</i> is in contrast with the term <i>low explosive</i>, which explodes (<a href="/wiki/Deflagration" title="Deflagration">deflagrates</a>) at a lower rate.
</p><p>High explosives can be divided into two explosives classes differentiated by <a href="/wiki/Sensitivity_(explosives)" title="Sensitivity (explosives)">sensitivity</a>: <a href="#Primary">primary explosive</a> and <a href="#Secondary">secondary explosive</a>. Although tertiary explosives (such as ANFO at 3,200 m/s) can technically meet the explosive velocity definition, they are not considered high explosives in regulatory contexts.
</p><p>Countless high-explosive compounds are chemically possible, but commercially and militarily important ones have included <a href="/wiki/Nitroglycerin" title="Nitroglycerin">NG</a>, <a href="/wiki/TNT" title="TNT">TNT</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinitrophenol" title="Trinitrophenol">TNP</a>, TNX, <a href="/wiki/RDX" title="RDX">RDX</a>, <a href="/wiki/HMX" title="HMX">HMX</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pentaerythritol_tetranitrate" title="Pentaerythritol tetranitrate">PETN</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TATP" title="TATP">TATP</a>, <a href="/wiki/TATB" title="TATB">TATB</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hexanitrostilbene" title="Hexanitrostilbene">HNS</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_physical_form">By physical form</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: By physical form">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Use_forms_of_explosives" title="Use forms of explosives">Use forms of explosives</a></div>
<p>Explosives are often characterized by the physical form that the explosives are produced or used in. These use forms are commonly categorized as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooper_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Pressings</li>
<li>Castings</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Polymer-bonded_explosive" title="Polymer-bonded explosive">Plastic or polymer bonded</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plastic_explosive" title="Plastic explosive">Plastic explosives</a>, a.k.a. putties</li>
<li>Rubberized</li>
<li>Extrudable</li>
<li>Binary</li>
<li>Blasting agents</li>
<li>Slurries and gels</li>
<li>Dynamites</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipping_label_classifications">Shipping label classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Shipping label classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shipping labels and tags may include both <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and national markings.
</p><p>United Nations markings include numbered Hazard Class and Division (HC/D) codes and alphabetic Compatibility Group codes. Though the two are related, they are separate and distinct. Any Compatibility Group designator can be assigned to any Hazard Class and Division. An example of this hybrid marking would be a consumer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firework" title="Firework">firework</a>, which is labeled as 1.4G or 1.4S.
</p><p>Examples of national markings would include <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Transportation" title="United States Department of Transportation">United States Department of Transportation</a> (U.S. DOT) codes.
</p>
<h4><span id="United_Nations_.28UN.29_GHS_Hazard_Class_and_Division"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Nations_(UN)_GHS_Hazard_Class_and_Division">United Nations (UN) GHS Hazard Class and Division</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: United Nations (UN) GHS Hazard Class and Division">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UN_transport_pictogram_-_1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="100" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/UN_transport_pictogram_-_1.svg/220px-UN_transport_pictogram_-_1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/UN_transport_pictogram_-_1.svg/330px-UN_transport_pictogram_-_1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/UN_transport_pictogram_-_1.svg/440px-UN_transport_pictogram_-_1.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UN_transport_pictogram_-_1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>GHS Explosives transport pictogram</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/HAZMAT_Class_1_Explosives" title="HAZMAT Class 1 Explosives">HAZMAT Class 1 Explosives</a></div>
<p>The UN <a href="/wiki/Globally_Harmonized_System_of_Classification_and_Labelling_of_Chemicals" title="Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals">GHS</a> Hazard Class and Division (HC/D) is a numeric designator within a hazard class indicating the character, predominance of associated hazards, and potential for causing personnel casualties and property damage. It is an internationally accepted system that communicates using the minimum amount of markings the primary hazard associated with a substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Listed below are the Divisions for Class 1 (Explosives):
</p>
<ul><li><b>1.1</b> Mass Detonation Hazard. With HC/D 1.1, it is expected that if one item in a container or pallet inadvertently detonates, the explosion will <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_detonation" title="Sympathetic detonation">sympathetically detonate</a> the surrounding items. The explosion could propagate to all or the majority of the items stored together, causing a mass detonation. There will also be fragments from the item's casing and/or structures in the blast area.</li>
<li><b>1.2</b> Non-mass explosion, fragment-producing. HC/D 1.2 is further divided into three subdivisions, HC/D 1.2.1, 1.2.2 and 1.2.3, to account for the magnitude of the effects of an explosion.</li>
<li><b>1.3</b> Mass fire, minor blast or fragment hazard. Propellants and many pyrotechnic items fall into this category. If one item in a package or stack initiates, it will usually propagate to the other items, creating a mass fire.</li>
<li><b>1.4</b> Moderate fire, no blast or fragment. HC/D 1.4 items are listed in the table as explosives with no significant hazard. Most small arms ammunition (including loaded weapons) and some pyrotechnic items fall into this category. If the energetic material in these items inadvertently initiates, most of the energy and fragments will be contained within the storage structure or the item containers themselves.</li>
<li><b>1.5</b> mass detonation hazard, very insensitive.</li>
<li><b>1.6</b> <a href="/wiki/Detonation" title="Detonation">detonation</a> hazard without mass detonation hazard, extremely insensitive.</li></ul>
<p>To see an entire UNO Table, browse Paragraphs 3-8 and 3-9 of NAVSEA OP 5, Vol. 1, Chapter 3.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Class_1_Compatibility_Group">Class 1 Compatibility Group</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Class 1 Compatibility Group">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Compatibility Group codes are used to indicate storage compatibility for HC/D Class 1 (explosive) materials. Letters are used to designate 13 compatibility groups as follows.
</p>
<ul><li><b>A</b>: Primary explosive substance (1.1A).</li>
<li><b>B</b>: An article containing a primary explosive substance and not containing two or more effective protective features. Some articles, such as detonator assemblies for blasting and primers, cap-type, are included. (1.1B, 1.2B, 1.4B).</li>
<li><b>C</b>: Propellant explosive substance or other deflagrating explosive substance or article containing such explosive substance (1.1C, 1.2C, 1.3C, 1.4C). These are bulk <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellants</a>, propelling charges, and devices containing propellants with or without means of ignition. Examples include single-based propellant, double-based propellant, triple-based propellant, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_propellant" title="Composite propellant">composite propellants</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid_propellant" title="Solid propellant">solid propellant</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_motor" title="Rocket motor">rocket motors</a> and ammunition with inert projectiles.</li>
<li><b>D</b>: Secondary detonating explosive substance or black powder or article containing a secondary detonating explosive substance, in each case without means of initiation and without a propelling charge, or article containing a primary explosive substance and containing two or more effective protective features. (1.1D, 1.2D, 1.4D, 1.5D).</li>
<li><b>E</b>: Article containing a secondary detonating explosive substance without means of initiation, with a propelling charge (other than one containing flammable liquid, gel or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypergolic" title="Hypergolic">hypergolic</a> liquid) (1.1E, 1.2E, 1.4E).</li>
<li><b>F</b> containing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_explosive" title="Secondary explosive">secondary</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detonating_explosive" title="Detonating explosive">detonating explosive</a> substance with its means of initiation, with a propelling charge (other than one containing flammable liquid, gel or hypergolic liquid) or without a propelling charge (1.1F, 1.2F, 1.3F, 1.4F).</li>
<li><b>G</b>: Pyrotechnic substance or article containing a pyrotechnic substance, or article containing both an explosive substance and an illuminating, incendiary, tear-producing or smoke-producing substance (other than a water-activated article or one containing white phosphorus, phosphide or flammable liquid or gel or hypergolic liquid) (1.1G, 1.2G, 1.3G, 1.4G). Examples include Flares, signals, incendiary or illuminating ammunition and other smoke and tear producing devices.</li>
<li><b>H</b>: Article containing both an explosive substance and white phosphorus (1.2H, 1.3H). These articles will spontaneously combust when exposed to the atmosphere.</li>
<li><b>J</b>: Article containing both an explosive substance and flammable liquid or gel (1.1J, 1.2J, 1.3J). This excludes liquids or gels which are spontaneously flammable when exposed to water or the atmosphere, which belong in group H. Examples include liquid or gel filled incendiary ammunition, fuel-air explosive (FAE) devices, and flammable liquid fueled missiles.</li>
<li><b>K</b>: Article containing both an explosive substance and a toxic chemical agent (1.2K, 1.3K)</li>
<li><b>L</b> Explosive substance or article containing an explosive substance and presenting a special risk (e.g., due to water-activation or presence of hypergolic liquids, phosphides, or <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">pyrophoric</a> substances) needing isolation of each type (1.1L, 1.2L, 1.3L). Damaged or suspect ammunition of any group belongs in this group.</li>
<li><b>N</b>: Articles containing only extremely insensitive detonating substances (1.6N).</li>
<li><b>S</b>: Substance or article so packed or designed that any hazardous effects arising from accidental functioning are limited to the extent that they do not significantly hinder or prohibit fire fighting or other emergency response efforts in the immediate vicinity of the package (1.4S).</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The legality of possessing or using explosives varies by jurisdiction. Various countries around the world have enacted explosives law and require licenses to manufacture, distribute, store, use, possess explosives or ingredients.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, the civil and commercial use of explosives is covered under the <i>Wet explosieven voor civiel gebruik</i> (explosives for civil use Act), in accordance with EU directive nr. 93/15/EEG<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> (Dutch). The illegal use of explosives is covered under the <i>Wet Wapens en Munitie</i> (Weapons and Munition Act)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> (Dutch).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="UK">UK</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: UK">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Explosive_Substances_Act_1883" title="Explosive Substances Act 1883">Explosive Substances Act 1883</a></div>
<p>The new Explosives Regulations 2014 (ER 2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-28">[28]</a></sup> came into force on 1 October 2014 and defines "explosive" as:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"a) any explosive article or explosive substance which would —
</p><p>(i) if packaged for transport, be classified in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> Recommendations as falling within Class 1; or
</p><p>(ii) be classified in accordance with the United Nations Recommendations as —
</p><p>(aa) being unduly sensitive or so reactive as to be subject to spontaneous reaction and accordingly too dangerous to transport, and
</p><p>(bb) falling within Class 1; or
</p><p>(b) a desensitised explosive,
</p><p>
but it does not include an explosive substance produced as part of a manufacturing process which thereafter reprocesses it in order to produce a substance or preparation which is not an explosive substance"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-28">[28]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"Anyone who wishes to acquire and or keep relevant explosives needs to contact their local police explosives liaison officer. All explosives are relevant explosives apart from those listed under Schedule 2 of Explosives Regulations 2014."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, numerous laws were created to regulate war related industries and increase security within the United States. In 1917, the <a href="/wiki/65th_United_States_Congress" title="65th United States Congress">65th United States Congress</a> created <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_federal_legislation,_1901%E2%80%932001#65th_United_States_Congress" title="List of United States federal legislation, 1901–2001">many laws</a>, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Espionage_Act_of_1917" title="Espionage Act of 1917">Espionage Act of 1917</a></i> and <i>Explosives Act of 1917</i>.
</p><p>The <i>Explosives Act of 1917</i> (session 1, chapter 83, 40 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a> <a class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-40-385" rel="nofollow">385</a>) was signed on 6 October 1917 and went into effect on 16 November 1917. The legal summary is "An Act to prohibit the manufacture, distribution, storage, use, and possession in <b>time of war</b> of explosives, providing regulations for the safe manufacture, distribution, storage, use, and possession of the same, and for other purposes". This was the first federal regulation of licensing explosives purchases. The act was deactivated after World War I ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the United States entered <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the Explosives Act of 1917 was reactivated. In 1947, the act was deactivated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_Truman" title="President Truman">President Truman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organized_Crime_Control_Act_of_1970" title="Organized Crime Control Act of 1970">Organized Crime Control Act of 1970</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_Law_(United_States)" title="Public Law (United States)"><abbr title="Public Law (United States)">Pub.L.</abbr></a> <a class="external text" href="https://uslaw.link/citation/us-law/public/91/452" rel="nofollow">91–452</a>) transferred many explosives regulations to the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Alcohol,_Tobacco,_Firearms_and_Explosives" title="Bureau of Alcohol, Tobacco, Firearms and Explosives">Bureau of Alcohol, Tobacco and Firearms</a> (ATF) of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">Department of Treasury</a>. The bill became effective in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Currently, regulations are governed by <a href="/wiki/Title_18_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 18 of the United States Code">Title 18 of the United States Code</a> and <a href="/wiki/Title_27_of_the_Code_of_Federal_Regulations" title="Title 27 of the Code of Federal Regulations">Title 27 of the Code of Federal Regulations</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>"Importation, Manufacture, Distribution and Storage of Explosive Materials" (18 U.S.C. Chapter 40).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Commerce in Explosives" (27 C.F.R. Chapter II, Part 555).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li></ul><p>
Many states restrict the possession, sale, and use of explosives.<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r998391716" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 35em;">
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2011-title18/pdf/USCODE-2011-title18-partI-chap40.pdf" rel="nofollow">Alabama Code Title 8 Chapter 17 Article 9</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://policy.mofcom.gov.cn/GlobalLaw/english/flaw!fetch.action?id=bc92580b-0101-41ca-9697-686811f3d7c1&amp;pager.pageNo=1#alaska-title-11-chapter-11-61-article-02-sec-11-61-240" rel="nofollow">Alaska State Code Chapter 11.61.240 &amp; 11.61.250</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.azleg.gov/ArizonaRevisedStatutes.asp?Title=13" rel="nofollow">Arizona State Code Title 13 Chapter 31 Articles 01 through 19</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.lexisnexis.com/hottopics/arcode/Default.asp" rel="nofollow">Arkansas State Code Title 5 Chapter 73 Article 108</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.leginfo.ca.gov/cgi-bin/calawquery?codesection=pen" rel="nofollow">California Penal Code Title 2 Division 5</a></li>
<li>Colorado (Colorado statutes are copyrighted and require purchase before reading.)</li>

<li><a class="external text" href="http://delcode.delaware.gov/title16/index.shtml" rel="nofollow">Delaware Code Title 16 Part VI Chapters 70 &amp; 71</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.leg.state.fl.us/Statutes/index.cfm?App_mode=Display_Statute&amp;URL=0500-0599/0552/0552ContentsIndex.html&amp;StatuteYear=2014&amp;Title=-%3E2014-%3EChapter%20552" rel="nofollow">Florida Statutes Title XXXIII Chapter 552</a></li>

</p>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://www.revisor.mn.gov/topics/?type=statute&amp;year=2014&amp;id=EXPLOSIVES%20AND%20BLASTING%20AGENTS" rel="nofollow">Minnesota</a></li>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Government">U.S. Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: U.S. Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/FM_5_250_Explosives_And_Demolitions_" rel="nofollow"><i>Explosives and Demolitions</i></a> FM 5–250; U.S. Department of the Army; 274 pp.; 1992.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/TM_9_1300_214_Military_Explosives_" rel="nofollow"><i>Military Explosives</i></a> TM 9-1300-214; U.S. Department of the Army; 355 pp.; 1984.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/explosivesblasti00dick" rel="nofollow"><i>Explosives and Blasting Procedures Manual</i></a>; U.S. Department of Interior; 128 pp.; 1982.</li>
<li><i>Safety and Performance Tests for Qualification of Explosives</i>; Commander, Naval Ordnance Systems Command; NAVORD OD 44811. Washington, DC: GPO, 1972.</li>
<li><i>Weapons Systems Fundamentals</i>; Commander, Naval Ordnance Systems Command. NAVORD OP 3000, vol. 2, 1st rev. Washington, DC: GPO, 1971.</li>
<li><i>Elements of Armament Engineering – Part One</i>; Army Research Office. Washington, D.C.: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Materiel_Command" title="U.S. Army Materiel Command">U.S. Army Materiel Command</a>, 1964.</li>
<li>Hazardous Materials Transportation Plaecards; USDOT.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Institute_of_Makers_of_Explosives">Institute of Makers of Explosives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Institute of Makers of Explosives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/gov.law.ime.explosives.1940" rel="nofollow"><i>Safety in the Handling and Use of Explosives</i></a> SLP 17; Institute of Makers of Explosives; 66 pp.; 1932 / 1935 / 1940.</li>
<li><i>History of the Explosives Industry in America</i>; Institute of Makers of Explosives; 37 pp.; 1927.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/clearinglandofst00matt" rel="nofollow"><i>Clearing Land of Stumps</i></a>; Institute of Makers of Explosives; 92 pp.; 1917.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/useofexplosivesf00inst" rel="nofollow"><i>The Use of Explosives for Agricultural and Other Purposes</i></a>; Institute of Makers of Explosives; 190 pp.; 1917.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/cu31924080100872" rel="nofollow"><i>The Use of Explosives in making Ditches</i></a>; Institute of Makers of Explosives; 80 pp.; 1917.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_historical">Other historical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Explosive&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Other historical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/farmershandbook00deptgoog" rel="nofollow"><i>Farmers' Hand Book of Explosives</i></a>; duPont; 113 pp.; 1920.</li>

<li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/historicalpapers00macdrich" rel="nofollow"><i>Historical Papers on Modern Explosives</i></a>; George MacDonald; 216 pp.; 1912.</li>
